class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">112</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">To solve the problem of large volume change and low electronic conductivity of earth-abundant ilmenite used in rechargeable Na-ion batteries (SIBs), an anode of tiny ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> nanoparticle embedded carbon nanotubes (FTO⊂CNTs) has been successfully proposed. By introducing a TiO<sub>2</sub> shell on metal–organic framework (Fe-MOF) nanorods by sol–gel deposition and subsequent solid-state annealing treatment of these core–shell Fe-MOF@TiO<sub>2</sub>, such well-defined FTO⊂CNTs are obtained. The achieved FTO⊂CNT electrode has several distinct advantages including a hollow interior in the hybrid nanostructure, fully encapsulated ultrasmall electroactive units, flexible conductive carbon matrix, and stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) of FTO in cycles. FTO⊂CNT electrodes present an excellent cycle stability (358.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and remarkable rate capability (201.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) with a high Coulombic efficiency of approximately 99%. In addition, combined with the typical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode to constitute full SIBs, the assembled FTO⊂CNT//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> batteries are also demonstrated with superior rate capability and a long cycle life.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5120%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLitao%2BYu%252C%2BJun%2BLiu%252C%2BXijun%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacsnano.7b02136%26title%3DIlmenite%2BNanotubes%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BStability%2Band%2BHigh%2BRate%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5129%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291058&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Hollow structures"> Hollow structures </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tiny+Nanoparticles&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_tiny%20nanoparticles">tiny nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ilmenite+Fetio3&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ilmenite%20FeTiO3">ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal%E2%88%92organic+Frameworks&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%E2%88%92organic%20frameworks">metal−organic frameworks</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hollow+Nanotubes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hollow%20nanotubes">hollow nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Full+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20full%20batteries">Na-ion full batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div id="ac_i1" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h2 class="article-section__title section__title" id="_i1"> </h2><div class="NLM_p">In the trend of developing low carbon dioxide emission and a more environmental friendly lifestyle, calling for more clean energy, such as solar radiation, waves, and wind, to replace the previous coal, oil and other nonrenewable energy have become very urgent. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a> While these sources require energy conversion and storage from off-peak electricity and release of the stored energy during the on-peak period, the most appropriate electricity supply system is batteries which have the advantage of efficiency, stability, and reliability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 5 to 9">(5-9)</a> Up to now, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in our daily life, such as computer, power electrode vehicles (EVs), portable electronic devices, and plug-in hybrid electric vehicles (PHEVs), but recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have come into our sight for energy storage, benefiting from the similar physical and chemical properties, abundant resource, and fairly low cost and have also been rehighlightted for next-generation large-scale energy storage systems (ESS). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Up to now, great efforts have been paid on searching suitable cathodes for SIBs, including Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2,</sub> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, and phosphates, which have been researched as cathode materials for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11, 12)</a> However, only a few kinds of anode materials are available for SIBs, although it is reported that many materials can be used as SIBs anode candidates, such as alloying type metals consisting of Sb and Sn, which could deliver huge volume change and results in cracks on the electrode surface and jeopardizes the safety of the battery; the conversion-type reaction M–X compound (M= metal cation, X= O, N, or S) electrodes suffer a high potential hysteresis and a low initial Coulombic efficiency; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 13 to 18">(13-18)</a> different from these two types, the insertion/extraction ones have also attracted the interest of researchers and the titanium-based anode materials, as the classical insertion candidates have become the beloved ones of scientists. For example, titanium oxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>), Na<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are now extensively investigated as advanced anodes for SIBs because of their negligible structure change during the sodiation/intercalation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 19 to 21">(19-21)</a> Unfortunately, the relatively low capacity of the titanium-based anodes greatly limits them as promising materials widely used in power EVs and PHEVs. Normally, the traditional improving methods such as structure and crystalline control, synthesizing materials in nanoscale size, and surface microstructure construction have also been widely applied in these Ti-based anode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 22 to 25">(22-25)</a> For example, Pierre and co-workers have successfully prepared the monoclinic phase of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> with continuous two-dimensional channels providing broader pathways and more space for the sodiation and desodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> In addition, as a kind of semiconductor material, the poor electrical conductivity and low diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> also restricted these Ti-based anode materials, which might be overcome by the introduction of heteroatom doping. Hwang <i>et al</i>. showed that the nanosized anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> doped with fluorine could improve its sluggish kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Zhang <i>et al</i>. also synthesized the N-doped yolk-like TiO<sub>2</sub> anode exhibiting a high reversible capacity of 242.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C and even at 20 C also maintaining a capacity of 115.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Moving beyond considering the doping effect, vast effort has been focused on the synergistic role of these foreign metal cations or anions to come up with better performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 and 25">(23, 25)</a> In these kinds of metal elements, iron was selected as the target material to synthesize ilmenite (FeTiO<sub>3</sub>) according to these following considerations. First, the mineral ilmenite has an abundant reserve in the earth’s curst and is widely distributed around the world (Europe, Africa, Australia, North and South America). The ilmenite has a high reserve exceeding 680 million tons in the world and a low price (the peak price was 250–350 USD/metric ton and in 2004–2008 only 80–107 USD/metric ton). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Besides, ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> belongs to the ABO<sub>3</sub>-type structure with both Fe and Ti ions of approximately equal sizes, and such bimetal oxides that contain two metal elements have great potential to possess excellent performance of ion storage properties, catalytic activity, or gas sensors. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 27 to 30">(27-30)</a> Considering these available values, herein, we put forward a compelling case for application of ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> in todays energy storage devices, such as SIBs. However, there is seldom research work on ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> applied as an anode material for SIBs, which may be due to the poor electrochemical performance of bulk FeTiO<sub>3</sub> and the nonflexible structure control of such complex ilmenite oxides. Herein, ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles embedded in carbon nanotubes (FTO⊂CNTs) were synthesized by a simple annealing process of TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated metal–organic frameworks (Fe-MOF). During this one-step annealing process, tiny ilmenite FTO nanoparticles were fully embedded in the <i>in situ</i> formed carbon nanotubes, and the obtained FTO⊂CNTs presented stable structure, high surface area, and excellent electric conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 31 to 33">(31-33)</a> When utilized as SIB anodes, they present an excellent cycle stability (358.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and remarkable rate capability (201.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) with a high Coulombic efficiency of approximately 99%. In addition, combined with the typical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode to constitute full SIBs, the assembled FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full batteries also demonstrated superior rate capability and a long cycle life.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The detail synthesis strategy of uniform FTO⊂CNTs is schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. First, uniform Fe-MOF (MIL-88-Fe) nanorods with the formula of Fe<sub>3</sub>O(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>Cl(BDC)<sub>3</sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O were produced <i>via</i> a simple solvothermal crystallization route with 1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid (1,4-H<sub>2</sub>BDC acid) and FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O dissolved in <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF) (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Methods">Methods</a> section for details). After the solvothermal process, uniform needle-shaped Fe-MOF nanorods were obtained. The template of Fe-MOF (MIL-88-Fe) made up of the metal ion Fe<sup>3+</sup> centers coordinated with organic complexes <i>via</i> strong chemical bonds delivered a marvelous, highly porous structure with high surface area and permanent porosity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 31 and 34 to 36">(31, 34-36)</a> Subsequently, these obtained needle-shaped Fe-MOF nanorods were well-dispersed in ethanol by sonication, then followed with the addition of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) and NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O under vigorous stirring. After the hydrolysis reaction of TBOT, the core–shell Fe-MOF@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods were prepared with the noncrystalline TiO<sub>2</sub> coated on the surface of Fe-MOF nanorods. Finally, the core–shell Fe-MOF@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods were heated in a quartz tube in order to obtain uniform FTO⊂CNTs. During the last annealing treatment, the organic complex BDC from the precursor Fe-MOF nanorods was carbonized at 600 °C in an Ar atmosphere, and the solid nanorod transformed into a hollow nanotube, while the Fe<sup>3+</sup> was carbothermally reduced into Fe<sup>2+</sup> and combined with TiO<sub>2</sub><i>via</i> the strong metallic bond to form well-crystalline FTO nanoparticles. As shown in the right section of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>, such fully encapsulated tiny FTO nanoparticles in well-conductive carbon nanotubes serve as an excellent electrode for Na-ion batteries, affording a high and sustainable capacity and robust high-rate capability.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration for the Fabrication of Uniform FTO⊂CNTs as Excellent Anode Materials for SIBs with High Capacity and Robust High-Rate Capability</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and the structure of the Fe-MOF and FTO⊂CNTs in detail are characterized by scanning electron microscope (SEM) at different magnifications. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b, the precursor of Fe-MOF particles shows a regular needle-like nanorod shape and high crystallinity (Figure S1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Fe-MOF nanorods hold a uniform side length of approximately 750 nm, a diameter length of 150 nm, and smooth surface clearly displayed in the high-magnification SEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d displays the final FTO⊂CNT products obtained by the coating and calcination process. Low-magnification SEM (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c) displays that these FTO⊂CNT particles still maintain the high monodispersivity and the same morphology of Fe-MOF template, while, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, the final products of FTO⊂CNTs remained with a uniform side length of approximately 800 nm and a diameter length of 200 nm. However, the solid nanorod morphology has been converted to a hollow nanotube, which could be observed from the broken particles. The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) were further utilized in order to investigate the microstructure of the uniform FTO⊂CNTs. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a and Figure S2 (in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), all FTO⊂CNTs present uniform hollow tube-like microstructures with wall thicknesses of about 30 nm, which is well consistent with SEM results (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). Higher-magnification TEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b–d) further display that almost all FTO nanoparticles were fully embedded in the thin wall of carbon nanotubes, and there is seldom FTO nanoparticles dispersed inside/outside of the nanotubes. The formation of such delicate nanocomposites is caused by the <i>in situ</i> generation of FTO and carbon during pyrolysis of organic BDC ligands. Moreover, HRTEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f) exhibit that these fully embedded FTO nanoparticles with sizes of 5–10 nm in the carbon wall matrix are well crystallized. The combined structure features of these FTO⊂CNTs (hollow tube framework, thin conductive carbon walls, and tiny fully embedded FTO electroactive nanoparticles) could effectively provide more free space for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, carry high loading capacity, and improve electron conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37-39)</a> The clear lattice fringes of 0.272 nm in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f could be assigned to the (104) lattice plane of rhombohedral-phase FeTiO<sub>3</sub>, indicating a good degree of crystallinity. Moreover, the elemental composition and distribution status of the as-prepared uniform FTO⊂CNTs are further demonstrated by the element mapping measurement (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). The dark-field TEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a clearly displays that these hollow nanotubes present an average wall thickness of about 30 nm and uniform inner diameter of approximately 140 nm, which is close to the average size of the precursor Fe-MOF nanorods. The uniform FTO nanoparticles with the average size of 5–10 nm embedded and dispersed randomly in the wall of hollow carbon nanotubes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c–f, the distribution of Fe, Ti, and O distinctly demonstrates the uniform FTO nanoparticles coordinated with each other <i>via</i> strong chemical bonds, with sparse elemental dots scattered between the void part verify that FTO nanoparticles disperse finely in the as-synthesized hollow nanotubes.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Low- and high-magnification SEM images of the (a, b) uniform Fe-MOF nanorod precursors obtained with a facile solvothermal crystallization route and (c, d) FTO⊂CNTs products transformed from Fe-MOF nanorods with simple TiO<sub>2</sub> coating and annealing treatment.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a, b) Low-magnification TEM images of uniform FTO⊂CNTs displaying the hollow capsule-like structure with 20–30 nm wall thickness. (c, d) Local high-magnification TEM images showing that tiny well-crystallized FTO nanparticles were fully embedded in the thin wall of carbon nanotubes. (e, f) HRTEM images of FTO⊂CNTs nanotubes clearly reveal lattice planes of FTO nanoparticles, verifying the well-crystallized and embedded nanoparticles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b) Low- and high-magnification dark-field TEM images of FTO⊂CNTs clearly displaying the tiny FeTiO<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles embedded in the thin carbon wall matrix. (c–f) The corresponding EFTEM elemental mapping images of FTO⊂CNTs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to demonstrate the crystallographic structure of the final product, the as-prepared FTO⊂CNTs were confirmed by X-ray diffusion (XRD) testing. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the black line stands for the product of FTO⊂CNTs; the obvious sharper and strong diffraction peaks suggested well-crystallinity of FTO nanoparticles. A series of significantly enhanced diffraction peaks at 23.9°, 32.8°, 35.5°, 40.6°, 49.1°, 53.6°, 56.9°, 62.2°, and 63.7° are observed, which are assigned to (012), (104), (110), (113), (024), (11–6), (018), (124), and (300) crystal planes of rhombohedral FeTiO<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS no. 75-1212), respectively. The carbon mass content in these FTO⊂CNTs was obtained by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and is presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The initial small weight loss of 0.15% at the range of room temperature to 150 °C is attributed to the physically adsorbed water molecules in these hollow FTO⊂CNTs. With the increase of temperature, the subsequent major weight loss of 25.47% from 200 to 700 °C is caused by the comprehensive effect of FTO and carbon oxidation with the products of TiO<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> (the composites of TiO<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> were directly confirmed by XRD analysis, as shown in Figure S3, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The TGA results indicate the carbon content in the final products of FTO⊂CNTs was about 28.69%. The surface area and the pore size distribution for these hollow FTO⊂CNTs were identified by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method. Their nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms in Figure S4a (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) exhibit a type III isotherm with a distinct hysteresis loop, which suggests obvious mesoporous microstructure with a surface area of 61.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. More significantly, the curve inset displays the pore size distribution calculated from the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method and indicates that these uniform FTO⊂CNTs are mesoporous composites connected with a broad bimodal pore size range from 30 to 60 nm. The large surface area and the mesoporous microstructure of FTO⊂CNTs made for more Na<sup>+</sup> and electrolytes to contact and reduce the distance of the Na<sup>+</sup> transportation, which are beneficial for improving electronic conductivity and presenting higher capacity. Raman spectrum of FTO⊂CNT (Figure S4b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows two sharp peaks at 1330 and 1584 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which correspond to D and G bands of graphitic materials, respectively. The intensity ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ≈ 1/1 indicates the graphene nature of the formed carbon with abundant defects and excellent electronic conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Composition characterizations of uniform FTO⊂CNTs. (a) XRD patterns indicating the pure phase of rhombohedral FeTiO<sub>3</sub>. (b) TGA curve of FTO⊂CNTs under O<sub>2</sub> atmosphere from room temperature to 700 °C, indicating the carbon mass content is approximately 28.69%.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore the potential of these uniform FTO⊂CNTs for Na-ion storage, the as-prepared FTO⊂CNT anode was assembled into Na-ion half-cells and full-cells. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a displays cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of FTO⊂CNTs in Na-ion half-cells for the first five cycles with a cutoff voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0 V at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. On the first cycle, the cathodic scan exhibits two reduction peaks located at 1.1–1.2 V and 0.1–0.2 V. The reduction peaks around at 1.1 V belong to the reduction of FeTiO<sub>3</sub> and the reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and the decomposed TiO<sub>2</sub> during the sodium insert process. The broad reduction peak at 0.1–0.2 V, disappeared for the subsequent cathodic scans, is mainly associated with the decomposition of the electrolyte resulting from the SEI film formation on the electrode surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref25 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref25 ref41" aria-label="reference 23, 25 and 41">(23, 25, 41)</a> During the anodic scan, two main oxidation peaks are obviously located at 1.0 and 1.8 V, which are mainly attributed to the charge of Fe oxidation states and desodiation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>. In the subsequent cycles, the reduction peak at around 1.1 V disappeared, and two peaks at 1.8 and 0.8 V are observed at the cathodic scan, which mainly corresponds to the redox reaction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe in the sodium insertion process. The <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns of the FTO⊂CNTs electrode after the second discharge to 0.8 V, 0.01 V and after the second charge to 3.0 V are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. When the FTO⊂CNTs electrode discharged to 0.8 V, the XRD peaks of FeTiO<sub>3</sub> disappeared, while some weak peaks assigned to Ti, TiO<sub>2</sub>, NaO<sub>2</sub>, Fe, and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> were observed, which confirms the sodium insertion process has actually occurred. When further discharged to 0.01 V, XRD diffraction peaks of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>, NaO<sub>2</sub>, and Fe turn more powerful, demonstrating more Na<sup>+</sup> insertion in the FTO⊂CNTs anode. While charged to 3.0 V, the peak intensity of NaO<sub>2</sub> decreased, and the peaks of Fe and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> disappeared, while the peaks of TiO<sub>2</sub> and FeO reappeared, corresponding to the oxidation reaction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> and Fe for sodium extraction process. Obviously, it is noted that the diffraction peak of Ti exited in all discharge/charge processes, which is similar to the previous work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Pan and co-workers concluded that sodium ions appear to partially reduce the rather stable oxide TiO<sub>2</sub> and form metallic Ti, NaO<sub>2</sub>, and reversible sodium titanate Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>, as explored by <i>in situ</i> XRD, <i>ex situ</i> XPS, and SEM analyses of electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The consequence shows that there is a certain ratio of Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> which could be reversibly deintercalated, and the reversible Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> is formed at the sodiation/desodiation process. The other ratio of Ti<sup>4+</sup> is restored to metallic Ti<sup>0</sup> in the first discharge state and exists at the subsequent cycles all the time, which is in contrast to the research of Passerini and co-workers, suggesting that a partial reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to Ti<sup>0</sup> was accompanied during the sodium ions insertion into the anatase lattices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43, 44)</a> With <i>ex situ</i> SEM, XPS, and EDX mapping analyses, the irreversible product of NaO<sub>2</sub> occurs at the first sodiation process and presents the flower-like morphology; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> this result shown in Figure S5a–c (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) is well consistent with SEM images. However, the diffraction peaks of the NaO<sub>2</sub> shown in the XRD pattern (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) are different from Pan groups’ amorphous NaO<sub>2</sub> conclusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves of FTO⊂CNTs anode in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) XRD patterns of the FTO⊂CNTs anode at different electrochemical stages.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">With all the preliminary results above, the cycle stability of these uniform FTO⊂CNTs was tested at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a presents the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of FTO⊂CNTs electrode for first, third, fourth, 50th, 100th, 150th, and 200th at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It is shown that the first discharge and charge capacities are 956.0 and 534.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a low initial Coulombic efficiency of 55.9%. The initial capacity loss may be mainly attributed to the irreversible reaction, owing to the formation of Ti, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and SEI layer, which is also revealed in the CV measurement. Subsequently, the obviously sloping plateaus at different discharge/charge voltage stages represent the electrochemical reactions in each cycle, and the voltage stages (1.0 V, 1.8 V/0.8 V, 1.8 V) correspond to the CV results. After presodiumed three cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a high capacity of 418.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can be delivered in the initial discharge process at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Along with the approximate 99% Coulombic efficiency, the uniform FTO⊂CNTs give a corresponding capacity retention of 90.0% after 200 cycles, which is much superior to other titanium-based anode materials both for LIBs and SIBs (see detailed information in Table S1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). SEM measurements of the FTO⊂CNTs anode after 200 cycles (Figure S5d–f, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) also clearly display that the 1D tube-like morphology after 200 cycles held perfectly. The rate capability of these uniform FTO⊂CNTs for SIBs anode was measured by cycling at various current densities. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, the discharge/charge curves present the sloping plateaus at different discharge/charge voltage stages even at 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and excellent restoration when the current density returns back. High capacities of 445.4, 390.3, 354.9, 307.3, 273.3, 233.2, and 201.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were achieved, respectively (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). When the rate decreased from 5000 to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 130 cycles, a reversible capacity of 386.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was delivered and with high capacity retention of 98.9%. Moreover, the ultrahigh cycling stability of the current ilmenite anode was also presented for 3500 cycles at a high density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e, they offered an initial discharge capacity of 217.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a maintained capacity of 210.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after long 3500 cycles, with approximately 99% Coulombic efficiency and 97% capacity retention. To our best knowledge, they have the most rate capability and cycling stability at high current densities among all recently reported titanium-based anodes (Table S1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) spectrum shown in Figure S6 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) displays that the charge-transfer resistance of the FTO⊂CNTs anode is only 30 Ω. Such a low charge-transfer resistance is beneficial to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, and the FTO⊂CNTs anode could present a higher reversible capacity. The low charge-transfer resistance, high specific capacity, and excellent rate capability of these FTO⊂CNT anodes can probably be attributed to their distinct microstructure features, such as 1D hollow electronic conductive carbon matrix and fully encapsulated/embedded tiny FTO nanoparticles, which could enhance the electronic conductivity, decrease the pathway for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electron transport, and enable stable, facile Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion/reaction kinetics.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance of uniform FTO⊂CNTs anode. (a) Voltage–capacity curves of these ilmenite-based nanocomposites at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V. (b) Cycling performance of FTO⊂CNTs at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Voltage–capacity curves at different rates from 50 to 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability at different rates from 50 to 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Ultralong cycle performance of an uniform FTO⊂CNT anode at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Along with the excellent performances in Na-ion half cells, these uniform FTO⊂CNTs were further tested in Na-ion full batteries, assembled with the typical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> nanoparticle cathode have good Na-ion storage performance, as shown in Figure S7 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), delivering a specific capacity of 133.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a high Coulombic efficiency of 99% and great rate capability. As the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode has obvious flat charge/discharge plateaus of around 3.3/3.5 V (Figure S7a, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/suppl_file/nn7b02136_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), the voltage window for FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells was supplied between 1.0 and 3.3 V. For the Na-ion full batteries, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a, there are two flat plateaus at about 2.1 and 2.9 V in the first charge process and a delivered large capacity of 965 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The first Coulombic efficiency is only approximately 50%, owing to the irreversible reactions of FTO⊂CNTs anode, the formation of SEI layer, and the activated process of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. The subsequent sloping plateaus located at 1.75 and 2.6 V and 1.5 and 2.4 V, respectively. This output voltage window is powerful enough to light a light-emitting diode (LED) (2 V, 20 mW) and work for a long time (inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b presents the cycling stability of the FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells at the current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles, a reversible capacity of 249 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved (the gravimetric capacity was calculated by the mass of FTO⊂CNTs anode). Moreover, for the demand of the modern electronic devices, the FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells rate capacity were also examined at various current densities. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d, they could present a reversible capacity of 421.4, 355.7, 312.1, 271.6, 248.1, 220.5, 185.7, and 307.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 30, 50, 100, 300, 500, 1000, 2000 and returned 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The typical charge/discharge profiles at these rates are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c, which more obviously displays the insertion/extraction process of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/medium/nn-2017-02136e_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Electrochemical performance of FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full SIBs. (a) The first four voltage–capacity profiles of FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cycled between 1.0 and 3.3 V at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; the inset digital image shows the light-emitting diode lit by the current full SIB. (b) Cycling performance at the current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Voltage–capacity profiles of FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> from 30 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability of FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> from 30 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136/asset/images/large/nn-2017-02136e_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.7b02136&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have demonstrated the engineering of the carbon-encapsulated structure of earth-abundant ilmenite as an ultrastable and high-rate anode for SIBs through a simple metal–organic frameworks-derived strategy. The achieved ilmenite FeTiO<sub>3</sub> tiny nanoparticles embedded in uniform carbon nanotubes have several distinct advantages including a hollow interior in the hybrid nanostructure, fully encapsulated ultrasmall electroactive units, flexible conductive carbon matrix, and stable SEI of FTO in cycles. Together with these nanoarchitecture features, as in our results, these uniform FTO⊂CNTs deliver outstanding cycling stability and excellent rate capability as used both in Na-ion half cells and full cells (assembled with the typical Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode). As FeTiO<sub>3</sub> is one of the most abundant mineral in the earth’s crust, the current research may discover further possibilities to develop energy storage solutions that are more sustainable, flexible, and producible on a large scale.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of Uniform Fe-MOF Nanorods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All the chemicals were directly used after purchase without further purification. The precursor of Fe-MOF was prepared by a facile solvothermal crystallization method. In a typical synthesis, 1.08 g of FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin) and 0.88 g of 1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid (1,4-H<sub>2</sub>BDC acid, Aladdin) were dissolved in 54 mL <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF, Aladdin) under stable stirring to form a clear yellow solution. Then 6 mL of 0.4 M NaOH (Aladdin) solution was added and stirred for another 5 min. After vigorous stirring, the final mixture solution was poured into a 100 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave. The autoclave was sealed and reacted at 100 °C for 24 h. After cooling down to room temperature, the samples were collected by centrifuging with DMF and ethanol several times and dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Synthesis of Uniform FTO⊂CNTs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as formed Fe-MOF nanorods were dispersed in 50 mL of ethanol by sonication for 10 min, followed by the addition of 0.56 mL of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT, Aladdin) and 0.28 mL of NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (Aladdin) under vigorous stirring. The reaction was maintained at 45 °C for 12 h to form core–shell Fe-MOF@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods. Then the product was centrifuged with ethanol several times and dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight. Finally, these Fe-MOF@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods were heated in a quartz tube at 600 °C in an Ar atmosphere for 4 h to obtain uniform FTO⊂CNTs.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The XRD pattern of the collected products was characterized by Rigaku D/max-2200/PC X-ray diffractometer at a scanning rate of 2° min<sup>–1</sup>. The <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns of the FTO⊂CNTs electrode in the sodium insertion/extration process were characterized at a scanning rate of 0.5° min<sup>–1</sup>. The morphology and crystal lattice of the samples were elucidated on a Zeiss Gemini DSM 982 field emission SEM. The TEM and HRTEM images were taken by a field emission transmission electron microscope (2100 F, JEOL Co., Japan) with the operated voltage at 200 kV. The content of the residual carbon in these uniform FTO⊂CNTs was investigated by TGA in an air atmosphere from 40 to 700 °C at a rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> by using a Q50 thermogravimetric analyzer. Nitrogen desorption and adsorption isotherms were evaluated at 77 K with a Quadrachrome Adsorption Instrument. The Renishaw Invia Raman microscope was used to obtain the Raman spectrum.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical properties of the FTO⊂CNTs were measured by assembling the CR2032 coin cells at room temperature (25 °C). The working electrode was composed of active material (FTO⊂CNTs), carbon black (Super-P), and polymer binder (polyvinylidene fluoride, PVDF) at a weight ratio of 70:20:10. The slurry was then coated on the copper foil and dried under vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h. The typical mass loading of active materials was controlled at 1–1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For the Na-ion half batteries, Na foil was used as counter electrode, and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5 vol % addition of FEC was used as the electrolyte. The electrochemical properties of the cells were evaluated by cyclic voltammetry from 0.01 to 3.0 V with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The charge and discharge cycling performances were carried out on a LAND-CT2001A battery-testing system. EIS measurement was evaluated at an open circuit potential with a modulation amplitude of 5 mV and a frequency range from 0.1 Hz to 100 kHz. For Na-ion full cells, the cathode was prepared by homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, carbon black, and PVDF in a weight of 70:20:10 with NMP, the slurry was casted on a Al foil and dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight, and the loading of active materials was controlled at 4.5–5.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The FTO⊂CNTs//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells were charged and discharged between a voltage of 1.0 and 3.3 V.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsnano.7b02136" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsnano.7b02136</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Supplementary XRD pattern of uniform Fe-MOF nanorods and TGA products of the uniform FTO⊂CNTs; low- magnification TEM images; BET and Raman data of uniform FTO⊂CNTs; SEM images of FTO⊂CNTs anode after 200 cycles; EIS spectrum of the FTO⊂CNTs anode for SIBs; electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> nanoparticle cathode;                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dunn, Bruce; Kamath, Haresh; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201503150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kopold, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Energy Storage Materials from Nature through Nanotechnology: A Sustainable Route from Reed Plants to a Silicon Anode for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Kopold, Peter; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9632-9636</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon is an attractive anode material in energy storage devices, as it has a ten times higher theor. capacity than its state-of-art carbonaceous counterpart.  However, the common process to synthesize silicon nanostructured electrodes is complex, costly, and energy-intensive.  Three-dimensional (3D) porous silicon-based anode materials have been fabricated from natural reed leaves by calcination and magnesiothermic redn.  This sustainable and highly abundant silica source allows for facile prodn. of 3D porous silicon with very good electrochem. performance.  The obtained silicon anode retains the 3D hierarchical architecture of the reed leaf.  Impurity leaching and gas release during the fabrication process leads to an interconnected porosity and the reductive treatment to an inside carbon coating.  Such anodes show a remarkable Li-ion storage performance: even after 4000 cycles and at a rate of 10 C, a specific capacity of 420 mA h g-1 is achieved.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3191"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bruce, P. J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li-O<sub>2</sub> and Li-S Batteries with High Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Li-O2 and Li-S batteries with high energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bruce, Peter G.; Freunberger, Stefan A.; Hardwick, Laurence J.; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-ion batteries have transformed portable electronics and will play a key role in the electrification of transport.  However, the highest energy storage possible for Li-ion batteries is insufficient for the long-term needs of society, for example, extended-range elec. vehicles.  To go beyond the horizon of Li-ion batteries is a formidable challenge; there are few options.  Here Li-air (O2) and Li-S batteries are considered.  The energy that can be stored in Li-air (based on aq. or non-aq. electrolytes) and Li-S cells is compared with Li-ion; the operation of the cells is discussed, as are the significant hurdles that will have to be overcome if such batteries are to succeed.  Fundamental scientific advances in understanding the reactions occurring in the cells as well as new materials are key to overcoming these obstacles.  The potential benefits of Li-air and Li-S justify the continued research effort that will be needed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600256"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, X. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, R. Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, L. C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Uniform Hierarchical Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@ Polypyrrole Nanocages for Superior Lithium Ion Battery Anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Nanoconfined Galvanic Replacement Synthesis of Hollow Sb@C Yolk-Shell Spheres Constituting a Stable Anode for High-Rate Li/Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Yu, Litao; Wu, Chao; Wen, Yuren; Yin, Kuibo; Chiang, Fu-Kuo; Hu, Renzong; Liu, Jiangwen; Sun, Litao; Gu, Lin; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan; Zhu, Min</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In the current research project, we have prepd. a novel Sb@C nanosphere anode with biomimetic yolk-shell structure for Li/Na-ion batteries via a nanoconfined galvanic replacement route.  The yolk-shell microstructure consists of Sb hollow yolk completely protected by a well-conductive carbon thin shell.  The substantial void space in the these hollow Sb@C yolk-shell particles allows for the full vol. expansion of inner Sb while maintaining the framework of the Sb@C anode and developing a stable SEI film on the outside carbon shell.  As for Li-ion battery anode, they displayed a large specific capacity (634 mAh/g), high rate capability (specific capabilities of 622, 557, 496, 439, and 384 mAh/g at 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA/g, resp.) and stable cycling performance (a specific capacity of 405 mAh/g after long 300 cycles at 1000 mA/g).  As for Na-ion storage, these yolk-shell Sb@C particles also maintained a reversible capacity of approx. 280 mAh/g at 1000 mA/g after 200 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C5CS00708A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Engineered nanomembranes are of great interest not only for large-scale energy storage devices, but also for on-chip energy storage integrated microdevices (such as microbatteries, microsupercapacitors, on-chip capacitors, etc.) because of their large active surfaces for electrochem. reactions, shortened paths for fast ion diffusion, and easy engineering for microdevice applications.  In addn., engineered nanomembranes provide a lab-on-chip electrochem. device platform for probing the correlations of electrode structure, elec./ionic cond., and electrochem. kinetics with device performance.  This review focuses on the recent progress in engineered nanomembranes including tubular nanomembranes and planar nanomembranes, with the aim to provide a systematic summary of their fabrication, modification, and energy storage applications in lithium-ion batteries, lithium-oxygen batteries, on-chip electrostatic capacitors and micro-supercapacitors.  A comprehensive understanding of the relationship between engineered nanomembranes and electrochem. properties of lithium ion storage with engineered single-tube microbatteries is given, and the flexibility and transparency of micro-supercapacitors is also discussed.  Remarks on challenges and perspectives related to engineered nanomembranes for the further development of energy storage applications conclude this review.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/nl5004174"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-supported Li4Ti5O12-C nanotube arrays with high cond. architectures are designed and fabricated for application in Li-ion batteries.  The Li4Ti5O12 nanotube arrays grow directly on stainless steel foil by a facile template-based soln. route, further enhancing electronic cond. by uniform carbon-coating on the inner and outer surfaces of Li4Ti5O12 nanotubes.  Owing to the shortened Li+ diffusion distance, high contact surface area, sufficient cond., and very good structure stability of the nanotube arrays, the self-supported Li4Ti5O12-C nanotube arrays exhibit remarkable rate capability (a reversible capability of 135, 105, and 80 mA h g-1 at 30C, 60C, and 100C, resp.) and cycling performance (approx. 7% capacity loss after 500 cycles at 10C with a capacity retention of 144 mA h g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201402484"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Si, W.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Single Rolled-Up Si Tube Battery for the Study of Electrochemical Kinetics, Electrical Conductivity, and Structural Integrity</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Si, Wenping; Moench, Ingolf; Yan, Chenglin; Deng, Junwen; Li, Shilong; Lin, Gungun; Han, Luyang; Mei, Yongfeng; Schmidt, Oliver G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A lab-on-chip electrochem. device was developed for the study of lithiation and de-lithiation of a single rolled-up Si tube, the detn. of the diffusion coeff. and elec. cond., as well as the characterization of structural changes.  Cyclic voltammetry of the Si tube exhibit sharp, better-resolved peaks compared with those of a planar Si film due to the enhanced diffusion effect in the unique tubular structure - this makes the rolled-up tube a candidate for an ultramicroelectrode or electrochem. studies.  Max. elec. cond. occurs after the lithiation of the Si tube.  After three cycles, the tube exhibits a highly wrinkled structure due to the strain-induced local deformation, which could be exploited to maintain stable charge/discharge cycling.  The single rolled-up tube battery described here is promising for research of voltammetry and electrochem. processes, and could also be used as local on-chip energy supply or for driving ultra-compact autonomous microsystems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/nn5048052"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.
