                10.1021/nn5048052</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D9%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D1198-1205%26journal%3DACS%2BNano%26author%3DX.%2BH.%2BLiu%26author%3DJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DW.%2BP.%2BSi%26author%3DL.%2BX.%2BXi%26author%3DB.%2BEichler%26author%3DC.%2BL.%2BYan%26author%3DO.%2BG.%2BSchmidt%26title%3DSandwich%2BNanoarchitecture%2Bof%2BSi%252Freduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2BBilayer%2BNanomembranes%2Bfor%2BLi-ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BLong%2BCycle%2BLife%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnn5048052&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b02136&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnn5048052&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sandwich Nanoarchitecture of Si/Reduced Graphene Oxide Bilayer Nanomembranes for Li-ion Batteries with Long Cycle Life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xianghong; Zhang, Jun; Si, Wenping; Xi, Lixia; Eichler, Barbara; Yan, Chenglin; Schmidt, Oliver G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1198-1205</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The large capacity loss and huge vol. change of Si anodes severely restricts their practical applications in Li ion batteries.  In this contribution, the sandwich nano-architecture of rolled-up Si/reduced graphene oxide bilayer nano-membranes was designed via a strain released strategy.  Within this nano-architecture, the inner void space and the mech. feature of nano-membranes can help to buffer the strain during lithiation/delithiation; the alternately stacked conductive rGO layers can protect the Si layers from excessive formation of SEI layers.  As anodes for Li-ion batteries, the sandwiched Si/rGO nano-architecture demonstrates long cycling life of 2000 cycles at 3 A g-1 with a capacity degrdn. of only 3.3% per 100 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y. X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced High Energy Density Secondary Batteries with Multi-Electron Reaction Materials</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">3</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1600051</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced High Energy Density Secondary Batteries with Multi-Electron Reaction Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen Renjie; Luo Rui; Wu Feng; Li Li; Huang Yongxin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1600051</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Secondary batteries have become important for smart grid and electric vehicle applications, and massive effort has been dedicated to optimizing the current generation and improving their energy density.  Multi-electron chemistry has paved a new path for the breaking of the barriers that exist in traditional battery research and applications, and provided new ideas for developing new battery systems that meet energy density requirements.  An in-depth understanding of multi-electron chemistries in terms of the charge transfer mechanisms occuring during their electrochemical processes is necessary and urgent for the modification of secondary battery materials and development of secondary battery systems.  In this Review, multi-electron chemistry for high energy density electrode materials and the corresponding secondary battery systems are discussed.  Specifically, four battery systems based on multi-electron reactions are classified in this review: lithium- and sodium-ion batteries based on monovalent cations; rechargeable batteries based on the insertion of polyvalent cations beyond those of alkali metals; metal-air batteries, and Li-S batteries.  It is noted that challenges still exist in the development of multi-electron chemistries that must be overcome to meet the energy density requirements of different battery systems, and much effort has more effort to be devoted to this.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/ic0700250"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sauvage, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Laffont, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure Na0.44MnO2 samples were prepd. via a solid-state route by tuning the synthesis conditions. Potentiostatic intermittent titrn. technique, together with in situ XRD measurements, showed the presence of 6 biphasic transitions in a potential range of 2-3.8 V (vs. Na+/Na).                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of Si core/C shell nanofibers and their electrochemical performances as a lithium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byoung-Sun; Son, Seoung-Bum; Park, Kyu-Min; Seo, Jong-Hyun; Lee, Se-Hee; Choi, In-Suk; Oh, Kyu-Hwan; Yu, Woong-Ryeol</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">206</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">267-273</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This paper reports on Si core/C shell nanofibers as an anode material for Li-ion batteries.  The Si core/C shell nanofibers are synthesized using co-axial electrospinning of Si nanoparticles in styrene-co-acrylonitrile core soln. and poly(acrylonitrile) shell soln. combined with subsequent thermal treatments.  The microstructure and elemental compn. of the nanofibers are systematically examd. using field emission-SEM, TEM and wide angle X-ray diffraction for confirmation of the cryst. Si core/turbostratic C shell structure.  In situ observation of contact-lithiation of the Si core/C shell nanofibers is carried out to investigate their mech. durability during the electrochem. reactions.  The electrochem. performances of the nanofibers as an anode are characterized by galvanostatic charge-discharge test.  Due to simple processing but a well-developed core/shell structure and good electrochem. performance, the Si core/C shell nanofibers fabricated in this study offer the potential to develop enhanced Si/C nanostructured composite anodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b00627"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, M.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase Titania Nanorods as an Intercalation Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Ki-Tae; Ali, Ghulam; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Yoon, Chong Seung; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Sun, Yang-Kook; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.09.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liao, J. Compared to graphite, the larger interlayer spacing of two dimensional VS2 can offer facile intercalation of lithium and/or sodium ions.  Aside from its natural metallic behavior, VS2 also possesses a high theor. capacity.  The composite NTO-VS2 nanowire arrays as an additive free 3D anode shows superior electrochem. performance compared to NTO.  After 50 (C/10) and 100 (1C) cycles, the charge capacities of NTO-VS2 are maintained, resp., at 298 and 203 mA h g-1, which are much higher than the values of 157 and 93 mA h g-1 obsd. for the same cycled Na2Ti2O5 electrodes.  The higher capacity, improved rate capability, and good stability of the composite NTO-VS2 nanowire arrays are due to the structural stability of the Na2Ti2O5 nanowire arrays and the higher capacity and cond. of the two-dimensional VS2 nanosheets.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05611F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banks, C.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon dots supported upon N-doped TiO2 nanorods applied into sodium and lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yingchang; Ji, Xiaobo; Jing, Mingjun; Hou, Hongshuai; Zhu, Yirong; Fang, Laibing; Yang, Xuming; Chen, Qiyuan; Banks, Craig E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped TiO2 nanorods decorated with carbon dots with enhanced elec. cond. and faster charge transfer have been fabricated utilizing a simple hydrothermal reaction process involving TiO2 powders (P25) and NaOH in the presence of carbon dots followed by ion exchange and calcination treatments.  Due to the merits of the carbon dots, doping and nanostructures, the as-designed N-doped TiO2/C dots composite utilized as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries can sustain a capacity of 185 mA-h/g with 91.6% retention even at a high rate of 10C over 1000 cycles.  It is interesting to note that the ratios of capacitive charge capacity during such high rates for the N-doped TiO2/C dots composite electrodes are higher than those at low rates, which likely explains the obsd. excellent rate capabilities.  In contrast to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries have gained more interest in energy storage grids because of the greater abundance and lower cost of sodium-contg. precursors.  The as-obtained N-doped TiO2/C dots composites reported here and utilized as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries exhibit excellent electrochem. performances, including substantial cycling stabilities (the capacity retention ratios after 300 cycles at 5C is 93.6%) and remarkable rate capabilities (176 mA-h/g at 5C, 131 mA-h/g at 20C); such performances are the best ever reported to date over other structured TiO2 or TiO2 composite materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/cm5035358"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Naeyaert, P.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrafast sodium storage in anatase TiO2 nanoparticles embedded on carbon nanotubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Lee, Joo-Hyeong; Abouimrane, Ali; Belharouak, Ilias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main disadvantage of using transition metal oxides for Na+-ion batteries is the sluggish kinetics of insertion of Na+ ions into the structure.  Here, we introduce nanosized anatase TiO2 that is partially doped with fluorine (TiO2-δFδ) to form electro-conducting trivalent Ti3+ as an ultrafast Na+ insertion material for use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  In addn., the F-doped TiO2-δFδ is modified by electro-conducting carbon nanotubes (CNTs) to further enhance the elec. cond.  The composite F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is produced in a one-pot hydrothermal reaction.  X-ray diffraction and microscopic studies revealed that nanocryst. anatase-type TiO2-δFδ particles, in which fluorine is present with TiO2 particles, are loaded on the CNTs.  This yields a high elec. cond. of approx. 5.8 S cm-1.  The first discharge capacity of the F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is approx. 250 mA h (g-oxide)-1, and is retained at 97% after 100 cycles.  As expected, a high-rate performance was achieved even at the 100 C discharging rate (25 A g-1) where the composite material demonstrated a capacity of 118 mA h g-1 under the 0.1 C-rate charge condition.  The present work also highlights a significant improvement in the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions when the material was charged and discharged under the same rate of 35 C (8.75 A g-1), delivering approx. 90 mA h (g-oxide)-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600173"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanding the Applications of the Ilmenite Mineral to the Preparation of Nanostructures: TiO2 Nanorods and their Photocatalytic Properties in the Degradation of Oxalic Acid</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tao, Tao; Chen, Ying; Zhou, Dan; Zhang, Hongzhou; Liu, Sanly; Amal, Rose; Sharma, Neeraj; Glushenkov, Alexey M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The mineral ilmenite is one of the most abundant ores in the Earth's crust and it is the main source for the industrial prodn. of bulk titanium oxide.  At the same time, methods to convert ilmenite into nanostructures of TiO2 (which are required for new advanced applications, such as solar cells, batteries, and photocatalysts) have not been explored to any significant extent.  Herein, the authors describe a simple and effective method for the prepn. of rutile TiO2 nanorods from ball-milled ilmenite.  These nanorods have small dimensions (width: 5-20 nm, length: 50-100 nm, thickness: 2-5 nm) and possess large sp. surface areas (up to 97 m2 g-1).  Dissoln./hydrolysis/pptn. is proposed as a growth mechanism.  The nanorods were found to have attractive photocatalytic properties in the degrdn. of oxalic acid.  Their photocatalytic activity is close to that of the benchmark Degussa P25 material and better than that of a com. high-surface-area rutile powder.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2013.06.103"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Glushenkov, A.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical reactivity of ilmenite FeTiO3, its nanostructures and oxide-carbon nanocomposites with lithium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tao, Tao; Glushenkov, Alexey M.; Rahman, Md Mokhlesur; Chen, Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. reactivity of the ball-milled ilmenite FeTiO3 and ilmenite nanoflowers with Li was studied.  The electrode assembled with the ilmenite nanoflowers delivers better electrochem. performance than that of the milled material during charging and discharging in the potential range of 0.01 and 3 V vs. Li/Li+.  The ilmenite nanoflowers demonstrate the capacity of ∼650 mAh g-1 during the 1st discharge, and a reversible capacity of ∼200 mAh g-1 in the 1st 50 cycles. The 1st discharge involves the formation of an irreversible phase, which is either LiTiO2 or LiFeO2.  Subsequently, the extn.-insertion of Li happens in a reversible manner.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen-doped carbon-coated Ti-Fe-O nanocomposites with enhanced reversible capacity and rate capability for high-performance lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Tao; Bai, Xue; Lun, Ning; Qi, Yong-Xin; Tian, Yun; Bai, Yu-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rationally designed composite materials could allow each component to fully play a part in their superior performance.  In this work, we designed and fabricated Ti-Fe-O nanocomposites coated with nitrogen-doped carbon to act as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries, in which Fe3O4 and FeTiO3 components provide high capacity, a small fraction of TiO2 improves cycling stability, and a nitrogen-doped carbon coating not only enhances electronic cond. but also alleviates the agglomeration and accommodates vol. change during cycling.  The electrochem. performance is assocd. greatly with Ti/Fe molar ratio in the products, and the composite with a ratio of 1 : 2 exhibits the best cycling performance (reversible capacities of 426.4 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and 321.7 mA h g-1 after cycling 500 times at 500 mA g-1) as well as outstanding rate capabilities (374.1, 347.5, 315.9 and 260.3 mA h g-1 at 200, 400, 800, and 1600 mA g-1, resp.).                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct Superassemblies of Freestanding Metal-Carbon Frameworks Featuring Reversible Crystalline-Phase Transformation for Electrochemical Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kong, Biao; Zu, Lianhai; Peng, Chengxin; Zhang, Yan; Zhang, Wei; Tang, Jing; Selomulya, Cordelia; Zhang, Liudi; Chen, Hanxing; Wang, Yang; Liu, Yang; He, Haili; Wei, Jing; Lin, Xiaocheng; Luo, Wei; Yang, Jianping; Zhao, Zaiwang; Liu, Yong; Yang, Jinhu; Zhao, Dongyuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">138</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">50</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">16533-16541</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-power sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with long-term cycling attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage.  However, traditional SIBs toward practical applications still suffer from low rate capability and poor cycle induced by pulverization and amorphorization of anodes at high rate (over 5 C) during the fast ion insertion/extn. process.  The present work demonstrates a robust strategy for a variety of (Sb-C, Bi-C, Sn-C, Ge-C, Sb-Bi-C) freestanding metal-carbon framework thin films via a space-confined superassembly (SCSA) strategy.  The sodium-ion battery employing the Sb-C framework exhibits an unprecedented performance with a high specific capacity of 246 mAh/g, long life cycle (5000 cycles), and superb capacity retention (almost 100%) at a high rate of 7.5 C (3.51A/g).  Further investigation indicates that the unique framework structure enables unusual reversible cryst.-phase transformation, guaranteeing the fast and long-cyclability sodium storage.  This study may open an avenue to developing long-cycle-life and high-power SIBs for practical energy applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201505918"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Z.                             cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1039/C5QI00226E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A New Sodium Storage Mechanism of TiO<sub>2</sub> for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Inorg. Chem. Front.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">3</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">464</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">468</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C5QI00226E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D464-468%26journal%3DInorg.%2BChem.%2BFront.%26author%3DD.%2BYan%26author%3DL.%2BPan%26title%3DA%2BNew%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BMechanism%2Bof%2BTiO2%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC5QI00226E&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b02136&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC5QI00226E&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A new sodium storage mechanism of TiO2 for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Dong; Pan, Likun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry Frontiers</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">464-468</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ICFNAW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2052-1553</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great interest for use as the next generation rechargeable batteries due to the abundant sodium natural resources and similar chem. of lithium and sodium.  TiO2 is an attractive candidate as an anode for SIBs due to its high safety, low cost, appropriate voltage platform and good structural stability during repeated charge-discharge processes.  However, the sodium storage mechanism of TiO2 for SIBs remains unclear, which appears to be different from the working mechanism in lithium-ion batteries.  This article highlights a recent report by Passerini's group, which successfully proposed a new sodium storage mechanism of TiO2 for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1039/C5RA14655K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jung, K. N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seong, J. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S. S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, G.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J. W.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-dimensional Nanofiber Architecture of an Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>–carbon Composite with Improved Sodium Storage Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">RSC Adv.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">5</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">106252</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">106257</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-dimensional nanofiber architecture of an anatase TiO2-carbon composite with improved sodium storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jung, Kyu-Nam; Seong, Ji-Young; Kim, Sung-Soo; Lee, Gyoung-Ja; Lee, Jong-Won</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">128</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">106252-106257</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2046-2069</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Titanium-based oxides have been considered promising anode materials for rechargeable sodium (Na)-ion batteries (NIBs); however, the slow reaction kinetics of TiO2 due to its low elec. cond. remains a crit. problem to be addressed for its practical application in NIBs.  Here, we report one-dimensional (1D) TiO2-C composite nanofibers for use as an anode for NIBs, which were fabricated via an electrospinning technique combined with optimized heat-treatment.  The TiO2-C composite nanofibers are composed of tiny anatase TiO2 nanocrystals surrounded by and/or embedded in the conducting carbon matrix.  When tested in an electrochem. Na cell, the TiO2-C nanofiber electrode retained a high charge capacity of 140 mA h g-1, even after 100 cycles at a c.d. of 60 mA g-1, and it exhibited excellent rate-capability (105 mA h g-1 at 3 A g-1), proving its feasibility as an NIB anode.  The superior Na storage performance of the nanofiber anode is mainly attributed to the unique composite architecture, i.e., a large no. of active sites per vol. due to the high aspect ratios and short lengths for Na transport through 1D nanofibers and TiO2 nanocrystals as well as highly conducting electron paths.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201401142"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bresser, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Buchholz, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Giffin, G.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1021/nl500548a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, C. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maier, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Embedded in Porous Carbon Matrix: An Ultrafast Na-storage Cathode with the Potential of Outperforming Li Cathodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">14</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2175</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2180</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nl500548a</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D14%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D2175-2180%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26author%3DC.%2BB.%2BZhu%26author%3DK.%2BP.%2BSong%26author%3DP.%2BA.%2Bvan%2BAken%26author%3DJ.%2BMaier%26author%3DY.%2BYu%26title%3DCarbon-coated%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25293%2BEmbedded%2Bin%2BPorous%2BCarbon%2BMatrix%253A%2BAn%2BUltrafast%2BNa-storage%2BCathode%2Bwith%2Bthe%2BPotential%2Bof%2BOutperforming%2BLi%2BCathodes%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnl500548a&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.7b02136&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnl500548a&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-Coated Na3V2(PO4)3 Embedded in Porous Carbon Matrix: An Ultrafast Na-Storage Cathode with the Potential of Outperforming Li Cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Changbao; Song, Kepeng; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2175-2180</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries are one of the realistic promising alternatives to the Li analogs.  However, neither theor. energy/power d. nor the practical values reach the values of Li cathodes.  Poorer performance is expected owing to larger size, larger mass, and lower cell voltage.  Nonetheless, Na ion batteries are considered to be practically relevant in view of the abundance of the element Na.  The arguments in favor of Li and to the disadvantage of Na would be completely obsolete if the specific performance data of the latter would match the 1st.  Here the authors present a cathode consisting of C-coated nanosized Na3V2(PO4)3 embedded in a porous C matrix, which not only matches but even out-shines Li cathodes under high rate conditions.  It can be (dis)charged in 6 s with a c.d. ≤22 A/g (200 C), still delivering a specific capacity of 44 mA-h/g, while up to 20 C, the polarization is completely negligible.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318a72668f9222a-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201503150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kopold, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maier, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Energy Storage Materials from Nature through Nanotechnology: A Sustainable Route from Reed Plants to a Silicon Anode for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">54</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">9632</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">9636</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.201503150</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Energy Storage Materials from Nature through Nanotechnology: A Sustainable Route from Reed Plants to a Silicon Anode for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jun; Kopold, Peter; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9632-9636</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon is an attractive anode material in energy storage devices, as it has a ten times higher theor. capacity than its state-of-art carbonaceous counterpart.  However, the common process to synthesize silicon nanostructured electrodes is complex, costly, and energy-intensive.  Three-dimensional (3D) porous silicon-based anode materials have been fabricated from natural reed leaves by calcination and magnesiothermic redn.  This sustainable and highly abundant silica source allows for facile prodn. of 3D porous silicon with very good electrochem. performance.  The obtained silicon anode retains the 3D hierarchical architecture of the reed leaf.  Impurity leaching and gas release during the fabrication process leads to an interconnected porosity and the reductive treatment to an inside carbon coating.  Such anodes show a remarkable Li-ion storage performance: even after 4000 cycles and at a rate of 10 C, a specific capacity of 420 mA h g-1 is achieved.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3191"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bruce, P. G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Freunberger, S. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hardwick, L. J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li-O<sub>2</sub> and Li-S Batteries with High Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">11</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">19</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">29</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nmat3191</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Li-O2 and Li-S batteries with high energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bruce, Peter G.; Freunberger, Stefan A.; Hardwick, Laurence J.; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-ion batteries have transformed portable electronics and will play a key role in the electrification of transport.  However, the highest energy storage possible for Li-ion batteries is insufficient for the long-term needs of society, for example, extended-range elec. vehicles.  To go beyond the horizon of Li-ion batteries is a formidable challenge; there are few options.  Here Li-air (O2) and Li-S batteries are considered.  The energy that can be stored in Li-air (based on aq. or non-aq. electrolytes) and Li-S cells is compared with Li-ion; the operation of the cells is discussed, as are the significant hurdles that will have to be overcome if such batteries are to succeed.  Fundamental scientific advances in understanding the reactions occurring in the cells as well as new materials are key to overcoming these obstacles.  The potential benefits of Li-air and Li-S justify the continued research effort that will be needed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600256"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, X. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1600256</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In the current research project, we have prepd. a novel Sb@C nanosphere anode with biomimetic yolk-shell structure for Li/Na-ion batteries via a nanoconfined galvanic replacement route.  The yolk-shell microstructure consists of Sb hollow yolk completely protected by a well-conductive carbon thin shell.  The substantial void space in the these hollow Sb@C yolk-shell particles allows for the full vol. expansion of inner Sb while maintaining the framework of the Sb@C anode and developing a stable SEI film on the outside carbon shell.  As for Li-ion battery anode, they displayed a large specific capacity (634 mAh/g), high rate capability (specific capabilities of 622, 557, 496, 439, and 384 mAh/g at 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA/g, resp.) and stable cycling performance (a specific capacity of 405 mAh/g after long 300 cycles at 1000 mA/g).  As for Na-ion storage, these yolk-shell Sb@C particles also maintained a reversible capacity of approx. 280 mAh/g at 1000 mA/g after 200 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C5CS00708A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Engineered nanomembranes are of great interest not only for large-scale energy storage devices, but also for on-chip energy storage integrated microdevices (such as microbatteries, microsupercapacitors, on-chip capacitors, etc.) because of their large active surfaces for electrochem. reactions, shortened paths for fast ion diffusion, and easy engineering for microdevice applications.  In addn., engineered nanomembranes provide a lab-on-chip electrochem. device platform for probing the correlations of electrode structure, elec./ionic cond., and electrochem. kinetics with device performance.  This review focuses on the recent progress in engineered nanomembranes including tubular nanomembranes and planar nanomembranes, with the aim to provide a systematic summary of their fabrication, modification, and energy storage applications in lithium-ion batteries, lithium-oxygen batteries, on-chip electrostatic capacitors and micro-supercapacitors.  A comprehensive understanding of the relationship between engineered nanomembranes and electrochem. properties of lithium ion storage with engineered single-tube microbatteries is given, and the flexibility and transparency of micro-supercapacitors is also discussed.  Remarks on challenges and perspectives related to engineered nanomembranes for the further development of energy storage applications conclude this review.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1021/nl5004174"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-supported Li4Ti5O12-C nanotube arrays with high cond. architectures are designed and fabricated for application in Li-ion batteries.  The Li4Ti5O12 nanotube arrays grow directly on stainless steel foil by a facile template-based soln. route, further enhancing electronic cond. by uniform carbon-coating on the inner and outer surfaces of Li4Ti5O12 nanotubes.  Owing to the shortened Li+ diffusion distance, high contact surface area, sufficient cond., and very good structure stability of the nanotube arrays, the self-supported Li4Ti5O12-C nanotube arrays exhibit remarkable rate capability (a reversible capability of 135, 105, and 80 mA h g-1 at 30C, 60C, and 100C, resp.) and cycling performance (approx. 7% capacity loss after 500 cycles at 10C with a capacity retention of 144 mA h g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201402484"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Si, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A lab-on-chip electrochem. device was developed for the study of lithiation and de-lithiation of a single rolled-up Si tube, the detn. of the diffusion coeff. and elec. cond., as well as the characterization of structural changes.  Cyclic voltammetry of the Si tube exhibit sharp, better-resolved peaks compared with those of a planar Si film due to the enhanced diffusion effect in the unique tubular structure - this makes the rolled-up tube a candidate for an ultramicroelectrode or electrochem. studies.  Max. elec. cond. occurs after the lithiation of the Si tube.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The large capacity loss and huge vol. change of Si anodes severely restricts their practical applications in Li ion batteries.  In this contribution, the sandwich nano-architecture of rolled-up Si/reduced graphene oxide bilayer nano-membranes was designed via a strain released strategy.  Within this nano-architecture, the inner void space and the mech. feature of nano-membranes can help to buffer the strain during lithiation/delithiation; the alternately stacked conductive rGO layers can protect the Si layers from excessive formation of SEI layers.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Secondary batteries have become important for smart grid and electric vehicle applications, and massive effort has been dedicated to optimizing the current generation and improving their energy density.  Multi-electron chemistry has paved a new path for the breaking of the barriers that exist in traditional battery research and applications, and provided new ideas for developing new battery systems that meet energy density requirements.  An in-depth understanding of multi-electron chemistries in terms of the charge transfer mechanisms occuring during their electrochemical processes is necessary and urgent for the modification of secondary battery materials and development of secondary battery systems.  In this Review, multi-electron chemistry for high energy density electrode materials and the corresponding secondary battery systems are discussed.  Specifically, four battery systems based on multi-electron reactions are classified in this review: lithium- and sodium-ion batteries based on monovalent cations; rechargeable batteries based on the insertion of polyvalent cations beyond those of alkali metals; metal-air batteries, and Li-S batteries.  It is noted that challenges still exist in the development of multi-electron chemistries that must be overcome to meet the energy density requirements of different battery systems, and much effort has more effort to be devoted to this.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1021/ic0700250"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sauvage, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Laffont, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.                         rightTab-cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.09.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liao, J.                         rightTab-cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-performance Na2Ti2O5 nanowire arrays coated with VS2 nanosheets for sodium-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liao, Jin-Yun; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2Ti2O5 (NTO) nanowire arrays coated with VS2 nanosheets (NTO-VS2) have been directly prepd. on a current collector as a 3D anode for Na-ion batteries.  Compared to graphite, the larger interlayer spacing of two dimensional VS2 can offer facile intercalation of lithium and/or sodium ions.  Aside from its natural metallic behavior, VS2 also possesses a high theor. capacity.  The composite NTO-VS2 nanowire arrays as an additive free 3D anode shows superior electrochem. performance compared to NTO.  After 50 (C/10) and 100 (1C) cycles, the charge capacities of NTO-VS2 are maintained, resp., at 298 and 203 mA h g-1, which are much higher than the values of 157 and 93 mA h g-1 obsd. for the same cycled Na2Ti2O5 electrodes.  The higher capacity, improved rate capability, and good stability of the composite NTO-VS2 nanowire arrays are due to the structural stability of the Na2Ti2O5 nanowire arrays and the higher capacity and cond. of the two-dimensional VS2 nanosheets.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05611F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banks, C.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped TiO2 nanorods decorated with carbon dots with enhanced elec. cond. and faster charge transfer have been fabricated utilizing a simple hydrothermal reaction process involving TiO2 powders (P25) and NaOH in the presence of carbon dots followed by ion exchange and calcination treatments.  Due to the merits of the carbon dots, doping and nanostructures, the as-designed N-doped TiO2/C dots composite utilized as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries can sustain a capacity of 185 mA-h/g with 91.6% retention even at a high rate of 10C over 1000 cycles.  It is interesting to note that the ratios of capacitive charge capacity during such high rates for the N-doped TiO2/C dots composite electrodes are higher than those at low rates, which likely explains the obsd. excellent rate capabilities.  In contrast to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries have gained more interest in energy storage grids because of the greater abundance and lower cost of sodium-contg. precursors.  The as-obtained N-doped TiO2/C dots composites reported here and utilized as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries exhibit excellent electrochem. performances, including substantial cycling stabilities (the capacity retention ratios after 300 cycles at 5C is 93.6%) and remarkable rate capabilities (176 mA-h/g at 5C, 131 mA-h/g at 20C); such performances are the best ever reported to date over other structured TiO2 or TiO2 composite materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1021/cm5035358"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Naeyaert, P.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main disadvantage of using transition metal oxides for Na+-ion batteries is the sluggish kinetics of insertion of Na+ ions into the structure.  Here, we introduce nanosized anatase TiO2 that is partially doped with fluorine (TiO2-δFδ) to form electro-conducting trivalent Ti3+ as an ultrafast Na+ insertion material for use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  In addn., the F-doped TiO2-δFδ is modified by electro-conducting carbon nanotubes (CNTs) to further enhance the elec. cond.  The composite F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is produced in a one-pot hydrothermal reaction.  X-ray diffraction and microscopic studies revealed that nanocryst. anatase-type TiO2-δFδ particles, in which fluorine is present with TiO2 particles, are loaded on the CNTs.  This yields a high elec. cond. of approx. 5.8 S cm-1.  The first discharge capacity of the F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is approx. 250 mA h (g-oxide)-1, and is retained at 97% after 100 cycles.  As expected, a high-rate performance was achieved even at the 100 C discharging rate (25 A g-1) where the composite material demonstrated a capacity of 118 mA h g-1 under the 0.1 C-rate charge condition.  The present work also highlights a significant improvement in the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions when the material was charged and discharged under the same rate of 35 C (8.75 A g-1), delivering approx. 90 mA h (g-oxide)-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600173"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C. At the same time, methods to convert ilmenite into nanostructures of TiO2 (which are required for new advanced applications, such as solar cells, batteries, and photocatalysts) have not been explored to any significant extent.  Herein, the authors describe a simple and effective method for the prepn. of rutile TiO2 nanorods from ball-milled ilmenite.  These nanorods have small dimensions (width: 5-20 nm, length: 50-100 nm, thickness: 2-5 nm) and possess large sp. surface areas (up to 97 m2 g-1).  Dissoln./hydrolysis/pptn. is proposed as a growth mechanism.  The nanorods were found to have attractive photocatalytic properties in the degrdn. of oxalic acid.  Their photocatalytic activity is close to that of the benchmark Degussa P25 material and better than that of a com. high-surface-area rutile powder.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2013.06.103"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Glushenkov, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. reactivity of the ball-milled ilmenite FeTiO3 and ilmenite nanoflowers with Li was studied.  The electrode assembled with the ilmenite nanoflowers delivers better electrochem. performance than that of the milled material during charging and discharging in the potential range of 0.01 and 3 V vs. Li/Li+.  The ilmenite nanoflowers demonstrate the capacity of ∼650 mAh g-1 during the 1st discharge, and a reversible capacity of ∼200 mAh g-1 in the 1st 50 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rationally designed composite materials could allow each component to fully play a part in their superior performance.
